5.35      Sixth, the insurance sector does not reach its potential, mainly because of the lack of
enforcement of obligatory insurance regulations, but also because two of the five insurance
companies continue to face difficulties (one of which is under interim administration). The
two social security institutions register deficits (at least in the pension branch). As a result,
neither the insurance sector nor the social security institutions are providers of long term
resources to the economy.

5.36      Weak access to long-term finance continues to hinder investmentby local firms. Only
two firms (in fact, commercial banks) have launched a bond issue on the regional financial
market which is thus little called upon to provide much needed long term resources. Bond
issues on the regional financial market are hampered by the requirementof a 100percent first
demand guarantee and by the lack of familiarity with the regional market. The banks are
limited by the transforhation ratio which allows the transformationof only 25 percent of short
term depositsinto medium and long term credits.

5.37      Finally, the legal and judicial framework does not provide an adequate support in the
enforcement of contracts and the recovery of credit. The process for taking and realizing
collateral is particularly weak. Mortgages remain the favorite guarantee, although the
acquisition of land titles remainsdifficult, if not impossible on vacant land.

5.38      Given such an environment, it is not surprising that respondents to the investment
climate assessment surveyt3identified lack of access to and the high cost of financing as the
two major constraints to the growth of their enterprises. Fifty-six percent of enterprises
identified access to finance and 57 percent identified financing costs as major constraints.

Governmentaction, medium-term vision

5.39      Financial sector reform has a high visibility in the GPRSF, as a contributor to both
growth and poverty reduction. The Government has put into place a technical assistance
project with World Bank financing to support its reform program. With a view to improve the
efficiency of the financial sector and increase its contribution to growth, the Government's
program encompasses banking sector reform (starting with the treatment of nonperforming
loans), strengthening the microfinance sector, restructuring the insurance sector and
promoting its business, and restructuring the two social security institutions. The latter two
measures should contribute to an increase in the availability of long term resources. In
addition, the reform program will take specific measures to improve the availability of long
term resources and the development of new instruments and institutions. Finally, important
crosscuttingmeasures deal with the reform of the legal andjudicial framework.

5.40      The Government commissioned an audit of the non-performing loans of all
commercial banks and held discussions with the commercial banks on the conclusions of the
audit with a view to develop a strategy to clean up the portfolios. However, the discussions
did not progress very much. The Government then decided to undertake efforts to: (i) require
the banks to implement the recommendations of the audit report with respect to internal
procedures so as to lower the risk of accumulating new non-performing loans (with the


l3 The 2004 Investment Climate Assessment provides insights into the perceptions of the financial sector by formal
enterprisesin Mali. The assessment sampled 196 formal enterprisesfrom the manufacturing,tourism, and transport sectors. It
also interviewed 1,I 34 employees.

                                                         26

B d n g Commission and the Treasury solicited to monitor the implementation of the
measure); (ii) assist the banks with the recovery of bad loans, by giving clear messages to
delinquent borrowers; and (iii) invite the commercial banks, one by one, to negotiate a
strategyfor cleaningup their portfolios.

5.41    The Government and public sector entities have recapitalized BHM through
conversion of depositsinto equity (for a total of CFAF 22.0 billion) and cash injection (of 500
million CFA). The Government facilitated a loan of CFAF 8.6 billion fiom all Mali-based
banks to BHM. Through tight liquiditymanagement, BHM has now accumulated about CFAF
5 billion of liquidity. It has also changed its computer/inforrnation systems and has prepared a
procedure manual. The objective is to have BHM fully functional, resume its lending
activitiesand lift all ceilings on deposit withdrawals.

5.42    The BIM is being restructured once more. Its information system is being modernized.
Operating expenses will be cut and portfolio quality improved. In the medium term, the
Government is intending to privatize BIM as part of the restructuring process. The final call
for bids is slated for June 2007, and adjudication is expectedin June 2008.

5.43    In the micro-finance area, in December 2005 the Government separated the
supervisory from the promotion activity,placing them in two separate entities. In view of the
concentration of the sector, the promotion activities are expected to encourage new entrants,
particularly in geographic areas with a minimal presence of microfinance institutions. The
Government intends to encourage the consolidation of individual institutions into networks,
so as to contribute to more efficient supervision. The restructuring and strengthening of
existing institutions and networks to bring them to self-sustainabilityis an integral part of the
program.

5.44    The Government will continue to take measures to enforce policy requiring insurance
policies to be issued by domestic companies, and will work towards improving the financial
situation of two companies currently facing difficulties. Roadside controls on vehicle
insurance policies, accompaniedby publicity campaigns, have proved quite effective and will
be repeated. The Government will work with the regional WAEMU authorities towards the
restructuring of two insurance companiesthat do not complywith prudentialnorms.

5.45    Mali is also reforming its social security system, through a process launched in 2004
with the revision to the code governing the public service pension fund CRM. The code
revision was followed by actuarial studies of CRM and W S (the social security institution
catering to private sector employees). This led to the redefinition of the parameters (i.e.,
contributionsand benefits) with a view to reduce present and projected deficits of the pension
system. The adoption of the new parameters requires further negotiations with unions and
retirees. However, the CRM code has been fully discussed with all stakeholders and has been
ready for presentation to Parliament since summer 2004. Among the important changes
introduced by the new code are: the possibility to modify parameters by decree rather than by
a law, and the de-linking of pensions from salaries and their linkages to inflation. The
Government has decided to present the draft code to Parliament before April 2007. The new
parameters would be adopted in 2007 by decree. With respect to INPS, a new draft should be
ready in 2007 and adopted by Government in April 2007.

5.46    A number of measures are called for to improve the availabilityof long term resources
whose scarcity hampers investment and growth. At the regional level, dialogue will continue
with BCEAO to modify the transformation ratio, whose current level at 25 percent constrains
the ability of banks to transform short term deposits into longer term credits. The supervisory
authority of the regional capital market is working toward replacing the obligationto secure a
100percent guarantee on each bond issue (which could increasethe cost of a bond issue by as
much as 2 percent) by a rating system. In addition, the Malian investment dealer is working
with three Malian firms to arrange bond issues on the regional market. The Government aims
to encourage the development of new institutionsand instruments,such as leasingand venture
capital,which would improve the availabilityof funds, particularly long term.

5.47    Finally, with respect to the legal framework, the texts relating to judicial experts and
warehousing, and modificationsto the civil and commercial code, the land tenure act and the
commercial codewill be adopted in 2007.

PRSC-1 actions supporting the vroaam

5.48    The PRSC-1 will support many actions that are part of the Government financial
sector reform program. Specifically, the operation will support: (i) in the banking sector, (a)
the treatment of the non-performing portfolio of the balung system; (b) the restructuring of
BHM; (c) the restructuring of BIM; (ii) the development of leasing activities; (iii) in the
micro-finance sector, (a) the adequate funding of the micro-finance supervisory and
promotion units,(b) the consolidation of the micro-finance sector; (iv) in the insurance sector,
(a) the restructuring of two insurance companies, (b) the enforcement of obligatoryinsurance;
(v) in the social security sector, the adoption of new laws and parameters for both CRM and
INPS; (vi) in thefinancial market, the launching of bond issues by Malian firms; and (vii) in
the legal andjudiciary area, the adoption o modificationsto important codes.

Expected benefits of the PRSC-I FSD actions

5.49    Overall, the various measures will result in a more efficient, dynamic and competitive
financial sectorbetter positioned to contributeto the development of the private sector and the
growth of the Malian economy.

5.50    As regards the banking sector, the treatment of nonperforming loans and the
implementation of internal measures to improve the granting and monitoring of loans, will
diminishing the risks of accumulating again bad loans. This will increase the efficiency of
banks and permit them to reduce their interest margins, thereby loweringthe cost of credit.

5.51    A restructured BHM, will be able to resume its lending activities and efficiently
recycle the savings of Malians from abroad. The privatization of BIM as part of its
restructuring, particularly if a dynamic strategic partner is found, will contribute to shake up
the current lethargyprevailing in the banking sector. It should be accompaniedby the entry of
new dynamic and efficient banks and the exit of existing banks too weak to sustain
competition.

5.52    In the micro-finance, the strengthening of institutions consolidated within competitive
networks will improve the overall efficiencyof the sector, bring it closer to self-sustainability

and allow it to better serve the needs of smaller firms that play a key role in the Malian
economy.

5.53    The strengthening of the insurance sector and the restructuring of the social security
institutionswill, in addition to improving the efficiency of these two sectors, make it possible
for them to provide longer term resources to the economy, so much needed to promote
investment and growth. Additional measures (such as assisting Malian firms in issuing on the
regional financial market, development of leasing and venture capital) will also contribute to
improve long term financing.

5.54    The amendment to the commercial code, the land tenure act and to the text related to
warehousing will facilitate the taking, registering and exercising of guarantees, thus
encouragingbanks and quasi banks in increasing their lending activities.

2B. Private Sector Developement

Key issues and challenges

5.55    Mali's objective of accelerated growth over the next five years at 7 percent per
annum-as      stated in the second PRSF-requires        a vigorous action on the part of the
Government to facilitate the development of the private sector, to diversifythe economy, and
to increasethe export performance. This calls for action to develop exports and to improve the
investment climate. The 2004 "Diagnostic trade integration study", the 2005 "Investment
climate assessment", "Doing business in 2007" report, and the 2006 Country Economic
Memorandum confirm the need to take action in these areas in order to improve private
investment levels and efficiency and boost growth with poverty reduction.

5.56    Under the 2005 "Economic Policy and Public FinanceManagement Credit", a number
of measures were initiated by the Government of Mali; and further action is warranted to
achieve higher export performance and to promote a business environment that is conducive
to accelerated growth. In the area of export promotion, action is being taken to increase
exports of crafts and agricultural products. With respect to the investment climate, early win
reforms were undertaken in the areas of business start-up and property registration. The
second PRSF spells out additional areas needing urgent action as reflected in the short term
action plan developed by the Presidential Investor Council in March 2006: (i) streamlined
licensing, (ii) flexible labor market, and (iii) improvedtaxation.

Government action and medium-term obiectives

5.57     In its quest for accelerated export development, Mali has sustained the reform effort
in the area of trade liberalization and regional integration. The open and transparent trade
regime is also reflected in the implementation of the WAEMU common external tariff since
2000. In the recent past, this reform effort was broadened to specific sector needs so as to
unleash the export potential in areas where Mali has a comparative advantage. It is in this
context that the Government decided to further enhance the export performance of the crafts
sector by eliminatingthe fees collected by the national museum and to strengthen the capacity
of artisans. In addition, the Government plans to enhance the export performance of other

non-cotton sub-sectors. Towards that end, the Government has started to establish norms and
standardsthat will allow agriculturalproducts to complywith export marketsrequirements.

5.58    Investment climate improvement is acknowledged as priority in the second PRSF
in order for Mali to achieve its objective of developing the private sector, particularly small
and medium enterprises, which will be the engine of sustainable growth. During the 2002-06
PRSP period, Mali undertook a number of refonns aimed at improving the business
environment, particularly the implementation of the regional business law (OHADA), the
adoption of an attractive investment code, and the improvement of access to land titles.
However, Mali has many challenges to tackle in this area as its ranking in the 2007 Doing
Business Report quite low ( 1 5 5 ~in~ 175 economies. To further improve the investment
climate, the PRSF lays out a comprehensive reform menu reflecting the conclusions of the
Investment Climate Assessment and the Doing Business reports: areas covered include
improvements in the legal and regulatory framework as well as in the judiciary system, labor
market flexibility curbing corruption, access to finance and infrastructure services, as well as
licensing simplification. Among all these reform areas, the Government is particularly
focusing, under the PRSC-supported reform agenda, on the followingpriority areas: business
start up procedures, corporate taxation and labor market flexibility. Implementation support
will be primarily provided under the ongoing Bank-financed Growth Support Project ("Projet
d 'Appuici la Croissance").

5.59    Business start up in Mali remains cumbersome as procedures are still numerous and
costly. A large share of the economy - close to 50 percent - remains in the informal sector;
and the tax burden on formal enterprises is rather high (50 percent of gross profit). The
Government has recently decided to streamline business creation procedures and cost.
Existing public-private sector dialogue mechanisms, including the Presidential Investor
Council, were brought to bear to identify actions that would be taken to ease the business
creation process, with a view to eliminating at least four procedures in the near future and
reducing the overall cost. In the medium term, the Government objective is to bring the
number of procedure to start a business down from 13 to 6, the number of days fi-om42 to 8
and to cut by half the cost of starting a business. In addition, the Government intends to
achieve institutional realignment so that the time spent by a private investor dealing with the
bureaucracy is minimized by settingup an operationalone-stop-shop center.

5.60    With respect to corporate taxation, the second PRSF indicates that Mali wants to
reach the WAEMU target of tax revenues representing a minimum of 17 percent of GDP by
2008, up from the current level of 14.7 percent. This ambitious target has major implications
for the private sector:such a performance requires that the tax base become larger and that tax
administration be more efficient, if the Government has not to increase the tax burden on the
existing enterprises, thus running the risk of eroding further the competitivenessedge. In the
meantime, there are areas of improvement regarding the rates and the tax administration that
Mali needs to tackle; and the Government has recently initiated a number of refonns aimed at
making progress in these areas. In the context of the new tax code ("Code Gknkral des
Impbts") enacted by Parliament in December 2006 and expected to be promulgated in early
2007, Mali has decided to remove the fee ("patente") on business creation. Furthermore, a
reduction in the property registration tax is being initiated in the new tax code, with plans to
fiutherreduce the cost from its current level-20.7   percent of the value of the property (one of
the 10highest in the world)--to about 16percent by 2009. This will be a trigger for PRSC-2.

5.61    An important issue that Mali needs to deal with is one of labor market flexibility;
this is at the core of the PRSF objectiveof generating employmentopportunitiesover the next
five years. To do so, the Government is planning to revisit its legal and regulatory framework
so as to eliminate unnecessary rigidities, particularly those related to hiring and firing
processes as well as to working hours.

PRSC-1 actions supporting the Government's medium-term promam

Export promotion

5.62    In the area of export promotion the Bank identified, in the context a value chain
analysis work, the existence of an anti-trade tax on crafts affecting low income population,
namely the artisans. This export tax, disguised under the name of "expertise tax" was
hampering the export potential of Mali in a sector with low margins but representing an
income opportunity for poor people. This situation called for a policy action to further
liberalize exports and implement pro-poor actions in this area. This would entail: (i) a policy
measure to remove the tax; (ii) an information awareness campaign for artisans, to be carried
out by the Ministry of Tourism and Crafts, to publicize removal of both the "assessment tax"
and the requirement for an export authorization from the national museum; and (iii) the
production by the national museum of an information guide for traders of culturalproducts so
that they know the criteriato comply with.

5.63    The Diagnostic Trade Integration Study carried out in 2004 emphasized the fact that
export diversification for Mali requires policies that would enhance its non-price
competitiveness, particularly in the area of norms and standards. To that effect, the Ministry of
Commerce and Industry needs to lead the effort of establishing and enforcing norms and
standards for export of agricultural products, followed with a capacitybuilding effort targeting
both producers and exporters.

5.64     On October 11,2006, an "arret6" removing the expertisetax on handicrafi exports that
was previously collected by the national museum, was jointly signed by the ministers in
charge of tourism and culture. Since then, an information campaign for artisans has been
initiated and the national museum will produce an information guide for traders of cultural
goods, who will pay a service fee if and when an assessment is requested fiom the national
museum.

5.65    In order to foster export diversification, the Ministry of Industry and Commerce has
initiated the preparation of regulations ("arr&t6s") establishing norms and standards for
agricultural export products. On November 7, 2006, the Minister of Industry and Commerce
signed an "arrM" adopting norms and standards ("Malinorm") for h i t s and vegetable, food
oil, and cereals. It is expected that more norms will be adopted by the national committee on
normalization and quality control which will be subsequently promulgated by the Minister of
Industry and Commerce as Malian norms.

Investment climate


5.66    As regards the investment climate, Mali ranks 1 5 5out
                                                             ~ ~of the 175 economiesreported
on in the 2007 "Doing Business" report in terms of ease of doing business. The findings

discussed in that report are in line with those of the 2005 ICA report. Major reforms are thus
needed to improve the investment climate so as to increase investment levels and improve
their efficiency. While the Government has started working on investment climate issues,
three major areas were retained for priority action under the PRSC-1, as they have a major
impact of private sector size and performance: business start up, corporate taxation and labor
market flexibility.

5.67    On August 11, 2006, the "single identification number" ('Loi portant institution du
numkro d'identification nationale despersonnes physiques et morales"), which will result in
the elimination of four procedures in business start up through the combination of five
separate registration requirements (tax administration, treasury, statistics and informatics,
social security, and labor) into one, was signed into law. For its full implementation, a decree
re-organizing the activities of theone-stop-shop center with a view to streamlining business
registration and to better service investor will have to be signed and the entity made
operational during the first semester 2007. This will be a trigger for PRSC2. It is also
expected that in the course of 2007, Mali should have computerized the commercial registry
so that company bylaws are accessible on line, instead of relying on paper publication that
takes up to three weeks.

5.68    As regards corporate taxation, the Government has agreed to initiate a marginal
effective tax rate study in the course of 2007 in order to align its corporatetaxation on the best
practice with the West African Economic and Monetary Union. Based on the study findings
and recommendations, the Government intends to implement measures to improve corporate
taxation in the course of 2008.

5.69    In the area of labor market, the Government indicated its readiness to start tripartite
consultations (Government, employers, and labor unions) by the end of 2006 on issues that
need to be addressed. A study on the labor market flexibilitywill be carried out in 2007, and
based on recommendations of the study, the Government would make all necessary revisions
to labor regulations in orderto improve the situation in 2008.

Expected benefits of the PRSC-1 actions

5.70    Progress on the above fronts would contribute to strengthening and expanding the
private sector; and creation of sustainablejobs in the private sector will be the main driver of
improved livelihoods for individual and their dependents, thus of poverty reduction. Specific
benefits are discussed below.

5.71    The removal of the export tax on handicrafts is clearly in line with Government's pro-
poor policies. It will translate into a reduction of export procedures for handicrafts and
improves the competitivenessof exports. Artisans' incomes have more potential to increaseas
a result of better margins and higher export volumes. For agricultural product exports, the
establishment and enforcement of norms and standards will result in non-price
competitiveness improvement, thus increasingMali's growth potential with a positive impact
on poverty reduction. These measures are expected to translate into at least 4 percent increase
in crafis and agriculturalproducts (excludingcotton) by 2009.

5.72    Improvements in the business environment are expected to help grow the private
sector in Mali, which is essential to resolving the employment problem in a sustainable
manner over the long term. Starting up a business will be improved as follows: (i) the number
of procedures will be reduced from 13in 2006 to 6 by 2009; (ii) time required will drop from
42 days to 8 days; and (iii) the cost is expected to be halved so as not exceed the annual per
capita income. As a result of the significant reduction of barriers to entry of firms, private
investment as a ratio of GPP should increase above its current level (14.7 percent of GDP),
and the tax base would get broadened. In terms of tax administration,further simplificationof
procedures will reduce the number of tax payments by the private sector from 60 to 15 by
2009. In addition to further simplification of tax forms and procedures, including
computerization, Mali may consider following the example of other countries in the sub-
region such as Senegal, Guinea Bissau, and Ghana who reduced their corporate income tax to
25 percent in 2005. Based on the recommendations of the business taxation, reforms aimed at
simplifying the tax system (number of taxes, rates, and administration procedures) are likely
to help reduce (i) the overall tax burden from 50 percent of gross profit to about 45 percent or
less, (ii) tax evasion, and (iii) corruption. This was the experience of tax reform in countries
such as Georgia and other central European countries. With respect to the labor market,
increased flexibilitywill be reflected in a reduction of (i) working hours index from 60 to 40,
and (ii) the cost of hiring from 27 weeks of salaryto 15 and of firing from 31weeks of salary
to 29. These reforms are expected to send good signals to the private sector and encourage
firms to hire more people, and this would result in at least 1000 additional permanent jobs
created in the formal sectorby 2009.

2C. Transportationand transitfacilitation

Key issues and challenges

5.73    An important factor for growth, particularly in land-locked Mali, is the quality
and reasonable cost of transport infrastructure, transport services, and transit
facilitation processes. The main issues and challenges in the transport sector were identified
as the outcome of the Bank transport sector policy dialogue, based on in-depth analytical work
from three recent Mali ESWs (Transport and Growth ESW in 2004, Trade Diagnostic
Integration Studyin 2004, and Country Economic Memorandum in 2006).

5.74     To develop its resources and economic potential, Mali needs an efficienttransport and
transit system to support trade and the country's products comparative advantage. However,
severe constraints make transport and transit costs too expensive (estimated at 30 percent of
value of imports compared to an average of 18 percent for comparable countries in the
Western Africa region), contributing to making imported goods expensive and exports less
competitivethan they could be. Such a weakness is a bottleneck for further growth, impacting
on all sectors of the economy, and is a limiting factor in achieving the poverty reduction
agenda.

5.75    The high costs in transport and transit originate from two major causes: (i) poor
condition and upkeep of transport infrastructure (roads, railroad, airports, rivers) and weak
management of its transport fleets (trucks, rolling stocks, airplane and barges); and (ii) an
unfavorable environment in which transport and transit operate due to several kinds of
obstacles (institutional and regulatory framework, taxes, customs, controls and abnormal

practices at numerous road blocks along Mali's main accesses corridors), although treaties and
conventions designed to ease transit exist but are not applied.

5.76    Among the main causes of the rather poor condition of the transport network is the
recurrent inability to organize, fund and manage the infrastructure once it is built. In
particular, this capacity to maintain the existing and future expanded road network ought to
have top priority in the strategy to strengthen the infkastructure in the sector. The fimding of
maintenance has to rest on a tamper proof resource mobilization mechanism which still needs
to be established for the road, airport and river networks. In order for further extension of
these networks to be sustainable, it is important that a well functioning maintenance funding
mechanism be in place. Otherwise, all new capital investment would be lost after few years of
unsatisfactorymaintenance.

5.77    Domestic trade also suffers fiom high transport costs which have increased on average
on the entire road network by 7 percent in real terms between 1995 and 2002. This
aggravation of transport costs is the result of a policy which favors upgrading of roads at
higher standards than necessary, including paving more roads than necessary given the low
traffic volumes, and in the process leaving insufficient resources for maintenance of the rest of
the network. In particular, the rural roads program has suffered from a lack of resources: an
estimated 90 percent of rural roads are in poor condition, severely hampering the potential
growth of agriculturalproduction and thus the growth of the entire economy.

5.78    The weaknesses among transport operators result from many causes that are tied to the
lack of know-how and untrained manpower, still often illiterate, with aging truck fleets and
un-adapted to transporting containers. But on the top of these inner weaknesses is the sector's
unfavorable environment which is subjected to various administrative burdens and tax
distortions penalizing formal enterprises while rewarding the informal sector, giving the latter
no incentive to become formal sector entities. Cargo loading onto trucks at the ports is subject
to a next-in-linerule, forbidding shippers to selecttheir road carrier, and resulting in importers
transporting their goods on their own trucks (a less efficient use of the truck resources).
Furthermore, transport is subjected to duplicate (and thus redundant) paper work and delays
during transit at the borders, due to customs agents asking for a re-issuance of the transit
document (the Carnet TRTE) and repayment of the customs guarantee. Clearance process
through customs at destination is lengthy and trucks are immobilized for long period of a
week or more.

5.79    Within Mali and in neighboring countries road block controls are numerous and are a
pretext for collection of extra-legal fees. A 2003 survey estimated that on the regional
corridors in West Africa on average, trucks were stopped at 48 check points, spent half an
hour at each stop, and paid on average US$240 per trip per direction. At a 100 vehicles.per
day during 300 days, unauthorized payments total US$7.2 million annually on the conidor
Mali 1Burkina Faso / Ghana. This situation has been aggravated by the Cdte d'Ivoire crisis
which has diverted international traffic from CBte d71voireto Ghana, Togo and even Benin,
lengtheningthe distance traveled by road. The additional transport costs are estimated at about
CFAF 10 billion annually due to the additional distance to reach these ports compared to the
distance to Abidjan. This explains why Mali's transport industry is unprofitable, as it is
inefficient and affects its self-financing capacity which in turn makes Malian operators
uncompetitivecomparedto foreign operators.

Government actions and medium-tern objectives

5.80    The Government is well aware that economic development and poverty reduction will
occur only if a well focused and aggressive set of policy reforms to ease up the present
unfavorable transport and transit environment are implemented, in addition to and preferably
ahead of furtherinfrastructureinvestments.

5.81    With the support of previous transport projects, the authoritieshave tried to streamline
the organization of the road management system by reducing the public sector's role in road
maintenance and to procure such serviceson a competitive bidding basis, as it is done for new
construction with the help of executing agencies AGEROUTE and AGETIER. The
maintenance funding is now organized on a tripartite basis by the Road Authority (RA) which
mobilizes financing, the other two parties being the transport operators and the ' National
Roads Directorateof the Ministryof Equipment and Transport (MET). One remainingissue is
the definition of the annual maintenance programs to be based on road surveys organized by
the Road Data Unit (MET).

5.82    While the Government has increased road maintenance resources from CFAF 5.6
billion in 2003 to CFAF 13 billion in 2007, has been unable to meet (in 2006) the agreed
target of 40 percent of road user fees as a share of Road Authority (RA) annual maintenance
budget, nor did it meet the 30 percent target for 2005. Those chargeswere to be collected from
road users through increased fees on petroleum product imports, an axle load fee on trucks,
and tolls at gates yet to be built. The percentage of the RA's own resources reached 20 percent
in 2005 and is expected to be only 15 percent in 2006 reflecting the increase in the
maintenance budget and no increase in road user fees. The Government intends to pursue its
objective of increasing the RA's own resources to 40 percent over the period 2007-08.

5.83    With respect to transit facilitation,the authorities, in conjunction with ECOWAS and
WAEMU, have agreed to operationalizethe interstate transit facilitation system (TRIE), long
overdue for implementation following previous adoption of the legal texts. To oversee this
process, a National Facilitation Committee was established in 2005 to advise on
harmonization of activities of the various authorities involved in overseeing the safety and
security of the road transit process. The authorities have also committed to implement a pilot
transit facilitation activityon the conidor linkingMali, Burkina Faso and Ghana.

5.84    Mali is continuing its road infrastructureprogram with external funding (from the EU,
WB, and ADB) to establish internal and external transportation links on main corridors as
well as rural roads. The investment program aims at linking rural areas to urban centers and
Mali to external markets.

5.85    The medium term action objectives for transport and transit facilitation are to (i)
strengthen the financial and institutional framework for the road maintenance program; (ii)
strengthen the fiscal and institutional framework to encourage competitiveness and service
quality of road transports; (iii) strengthen transit facilitation; and (iv) improve governance on
Mali's transport corridors.

5.86    The actions contemplated include implementing some decisions immediately, in
particular with respect to transit and customs facilitation to ease the turnaround time of the

transport fleet, to reduce unutilized time, capital invested, and higher-than-required operating
costs. Other actions will require setting up new rules and mechanisms to ensure regular
funding of road maintenance through new revenue sources andlor more direct collection of
users fees on imported petroleum products. Among those decisions, customs have agreed to
implement the Customs Code provision whereby trucks have to be released within three days
(72 hours) if the shipment has not been cleared. Further, the perception of customs duties will
be made possible during week-end as the Director of the Treasury has decided that it is
important to enable clearing customs without interruptions stemming from a misalignment of
working hours. Other actions require further study as a first step. This includes the action to:
(i) equalize the taxation treatment of formal and informal transport operators, to eliminate
present distortions encouraging the less performing operators; and (ii) establish fieight
exchange markets by product group to enable better information on services, and
strengthening of service quality, offered by transporters to shippers. Substantial effort will be
required to apply the interstate transit rules agreed within ECOWAS and WAEMU, but which
are not yet operational, starting with the measures that can be gradually initiated within Mali's
borders.

PRSC-1 actions supportingthe Government's medium-term program;

5.87    The PRSC-1 would pursue key policy measures to address quality and cost concerns in
transport and transit facilitation services, and ensure public interventions in the sector are
effective and efficient. As such, the PRSC focuses on the non-infrastructure investment
aspects of the transport and transit sector strategy.


5.88    The operation would contribute to reducing transport and transit costs, by actions
toward (i) ensuring regular funding of road maintenance; (ii) strengthening the incentive
framework for the private sector transport operators and shippers; (iii) improving customs
administration and controls; and (iv) operationalizing the regional transit facilitation rules.

5.89    Ensuring adequate resources and financing mechanism for road maintenance. As
started under the EPPFMC, emphasis of the PRSC-1 is on ensuring that funding of routine
road maintenance becomes more dependable, by extending the source of financingbeyond the
government budget to include road user fees ("own funds" of the Road Authority), and by
achieving a regular and timely transfer of those resources to the Road Authority. So far, the
"own funds" comprise a user fee on petroleum imports (at present CFAF 3 per liter of a pump
price of about CFAF 610), an axle load charge on trucks proportional to the carrying capacity.
A road usage toll fee to be collected at a few locations on the most heavily traveled roads, is
planned but not yet effective. In the PRSC-1, the objective will be to secure the flow of user
fees collected on petroleum product imports by creating a separate line item in the petroleum
product price structure that will be collected independently of the petroleum product import
tax (TIPP). This will ensure that the funds collected will be passed on regularly to the Road
Authority, thereby avoiding the complexity, delays and confusion in the present collection of
this fee as part of the TIPP. Furthermore,the increase in the road user fee required to fund the
road maintenance budget will easier since this fee will be independent of fiscal revenues.

5.90    Strengthening the incentive framework for private sector transport operators and
shippers would include the carrying out of: (i) a study on road transport taxation with a view

to adopt a plan for eliminating tax distortions among the formal and informal transport
operators and alleviate undue tax burden on firms; and (ii) a study to pilot freight exchange
markets to more effectively link transport demand and supply offers with a view to
eliminating the current first-come-first-servedpractice (whereby shippershave to use the next
transporter in the queue rather than being able to select a transporter based on criteria) and
thus to strengtheningthe efficiency, qualityand competitivenessof transport services.

5.91    Improving customs administration and controls would include applying existing but
not operational customs procedures, with a view to reduce the transit time through customs.
This would include: (i) making greater use of the "green" channel whereby goods satisfying
the low risk criteria would be admitted without custom inspection; (ii) implementing the
recommendations of external and internal audits on whether the customsfacilitationprocess is
indeed being accelerated; (iii) ensuring that trucks, whose goods would require more than 72
hours to clear customs, would be freed by unloading the cargo to secured storage areas; and
(iv) enabling the payment of customs duties during the week-end by having the Treasuryopen
its customs office at those times.

5.92    Operationalizing regional transit facilitation rules. Measures would include the
implementation and respect of legislation and operating procedures, with a view to eliminate
the extra-legal road blocks on road corridors and associated high charges. This would include:
(i) ensuring that each department concerned (gendarmerie, police, customs, transport, health,
forestry, culture, local and decentralized administrations) implements and respects the
legislation whereby trucks in transit having satisfied all requirements under the ECOWAS
TRIE agreement (i.e., verified bill of lading, certified sealed trucks, and paid custom
guarantee fee) are not stopped with the exception of three locations: at origin (in the port or at
the plant), at the border of the transit countries,and at destinationin the custom areas (in Mali
or at the exportingport); (ii) organizing an awarenesscampaign informingthe personnel of all
concerned government agencies as well as transporters that the law is to be enforced; (iii)
strengthening the mandate and hnding of the National Facilitation Committee, thereby
transforming the Committee into an operational unit to monitor the extent of implementation
of the roadblock suppression measures; and (iv) recording the indicatorsof trucks rotations on
Mali's access corridors through the Observatory of Extralegal Practices which will receive
additional funding to carry out its mission. Its findings will be auditedjointly by the MET and
MEF in view of preparing correctiveactions as required.

Expected benefits of the PRSC-1 actions

5.93    The PRSC-1 benefits would be modest since the actions are early phases of the 2006-
2008 sector program. The immediatebenefitswould be: (i) the lowering of transport operating
costs from the more regular financing and implementation of road maintenance; (ii) more
efficient utilization of truck fleets due to faster customsclearance of shipmentreleasing trucks
faster; and (iii) an increase in profitability of trucking operations due to the elimination of
extra-legal fees collected at road blocks.

5.94    The impact would be measured in terms of the share of roads maintained in the
network, tax pressure on transport operators before and after the taxation study results are
applied. Other indicators will measure the number of transport contracts awarded through
commercial bidding in the freight exchange markets. Other indicators will register the

turnaround time of trucks between Mali and seaports, and duration of processing at the
customs clearance centers. The responsible parties in charge of tracking the results have been
clearlyidentified and are shown in the policy matrix (Annex 3).

5.95     Over the medium term the expected benefits would be substantial. By lowering the
cost of transport and transit facilitation services, the PRSC actions would contribute to
spurring growth in all sectors of Mali's economy. Better maintained roads would reduce
transport costs which should translate into lower market prices of food and other products.
Harmonizing the tax treatment of formal and informal transport operators and developing
freight markets would improve the quality of transport services, contribute to increasing
profits and thereby enable better management and modernization of the transport fleet
(includinggreatertransition to containertechnology) as well as lower transport prices.

20. Energy Sector

Key issues and challenges

5.96     Domestic energy resources of Mali consist mainly of biomass, hydro, solar and wind
energy. Only biomass for household energy and hydro for electricity generation are really
exploited. The country's hydroelectric potential, based on the Senegal and Niger River
systems, is estimated at about 1,000 MW capable of producing 5,000 GWh on average. Of
this potential, only about 250 MW have been developed so far, at the Selingud and Sotuba
dams on the Niger River and the Manantali dam on the Senegal River. Although oil
explorationwork is activelyon-going, it is not clear at this point whether Mali has significant
fossil fuel reserves that are economically exploitable. Petroleum products are so far entirely
imported.

5.97     Based on 2005 figures, only about 13percent of the populationhas access to electricity
in the country. In urban areas the access rate is about 30 percent and it is barely 2 percent in
rural areas. These low access rates are a major constraint for business development,
productivity, and competitiveness. It was expected through the privatization of the water and
electricityutility EDM SA {Energie du Mali Socibtk Anonyme) in 2000 that private financing
would be injected in the sectors to provide system reliability and access expansion'4. The
commissioning of the Manantali hydroelectric plant in 2002, investments to reinforce
transmission and distribution networks, and improved operational efficiency, contributed to
meet demand mainly in Bamako and in selected areas connected to the main grid. However,
implementation of the concession contract on electricity (between 2000 and 2005) was
difficult as parties to the contract did not have the same interpretations of tariff-setting
mechanisms and contractual investment obligations. EDM SA found itself in 2006 in a poor
financial situation in a context of growing demand for electricity services driven by rapid
urbanization while domesticelectricitygeneration capacitywas constrained.

5.98     To meet the country's goal of accelerated and shared growth four main challenges
should be tackled in the energy sector: (i) restoring EDM SA financial viability and improving


l4After a competitive bidding process, the GOM signed with SAUR InternationaYIndustrial Promotion Service
(SAUROPS) two concession contracts on electricity and water for 20 years on November 21, 2000, with the
possibility of an extension for 5 additionalyears. EDM became EDM SA with majority shareholders SAUR/IPS
owning 60%and the State of Mali holding 40%of shares.

its operational efficiency; (ii) ensuring an adequate supply of electricity to meet a growing
demand of about 10percent per annurn; (iii) a sustained energy access expansion in rural and
peri-urban areas to support productive activities and to enhance social programs; and (iv) a
timely implementation of electricity interconnectionswith neighboring countries.

5.99      Restoring EDM SA financial viability and improving its operational efficiency:
Internal inefficiencies and the recent dramatic rise of international oil prices left EDM SA in
severe financial difficulties. EDM SA has accumulated large payment arrears towards its
energy and fuel suppliersas well as debts towards commercialbanks. The GOM decision not
to adjust tariff levels since they were reduced in 2004 has been a major factor in preventing
EDM SA to achieve cost recovery. Since 2006, EDM SA is in a situation where it can only
meet additional demand for electricitywith thermal generation. Given high fuel costs in Mali
and distribution losses, electricity tariffs (CFAF 87 per kwh on average) cover only about 60
percent of the variable costs of thermal generation. As a result, and until new interconnections
with neighboring countries come into service, the unit cost of generation for EDM SA will
mechanicallyincrease year after year. If tariffs are left unchanged, EDM SA will suffer from a
growing operating deficit. According to estimates provided by the GOM, the EDM operating
deficit that would need to be covered would grow from CFAF 13.7 billions (US$28 millions)
to CFAF 22.1 billions in 2009 (US$45 millions )I5.                In order for EDM SA to ensure continuity
of electricityserviceprovision and prevent widespread load shedding or even a collapse of the
system, there is an urgent need to restore EDM7sfinancial viability. A cash injection by the
GOM could be part of the solution at least for the short term; however no disposition to that
effect seems to have been included in the 2007 budget. Nonetheless, this would be acceptable
only as a temporary measure intended to accompanya comprehensiverestructuring process. A
permanent subsidy to cover EDM SA current operating deficit would entail an increasing
drain on public finances in a context where significant government and donor support to
financeEDM expansion investmentsare also needed.

5.100 A logical step to bridge the forecast operating deficit and achieve cost recovery would
be to increase tariffs revenue. However, compared to current levels, the average revenue per
kwh sold would need to increaseby about 21 percent in 2007 and 29 percent in 2009 in order
to close the gap between current costs and revenues. This highlights the critical need for
rigorous actionsto improve EDM SA operational efficiency. The EDM SA management team
in place since the departure of the strategicpartner (SAUR) in 2005 must be credited with cost
reductions in several areas (expatriate salaries, management fees, technical assistance and
other services). Going forward, for the short and medium term, the key levers to reduce costs
seem to be (i) further reduction of technical and commercial losses (about 23.4 percent in
2005 and improving since 2003), (ii) reducing thermal generation costs for instance by
converting current diesel units to heavy fuel oil, and (iii) implementing an aggressive demand-
side management program. Overall, such measures if successfully implemented would reduce
EDM SA operating deficit and contribute to delay, but not prevent, power shortages
susceptibleto have negative impactson an already fragileeconomy.




15Based on available data, the World Bank Energy team considers that this estimate is realistic with current oil
prices. It must be noted that together with future oil prices, the volume of hydroelectric generation is a significant
factor of uncertainty.

5.101 Ensuring an adequate supply of electricity to meet a growing demand: In order to
achieve and sustain its objectivesfor the sector (accessexpansion and affordability), the GOM
needs to implement an investment policy able to respond to short term needs but also to
medium-to-long-term planning and least cost expansion objectives. A power generation
deficit ranging fiom 15 to 45 MW is estimatedbetween 2007 and 2009. It results from a low
level of investmentsduring the implementation of EDM SA electricityconcession contract as
well as fiom poor rainfall levels in 2006 pushing down the Manantali water retention level to
about 5 meters of its normal level. Mali is a beneficiary of the Second West Africa Power
Pool Adaptable Project Loan (WAPP APL 2; Credit 4216-MLQ approved by the Board in
June 2006 under which the 60 MW Fklou Hydroelectric Plant is being developed, at a site 200
krn downstream of the existing Manantali hydro plant. With expected plant commissioningin
2009, Mali's share will be at least 33 percent of the plant's projected annual electricity output
of 320-350 GWh. The interconnection with Cote d71voireand with Ghana through Burkina is
not expected to be completed before 2009-2010. In the short term (2007-2009), Mali has to
rely on additional thermal units to meet its growing demand for electricity. Through financing
from India, thermal units of a capacity of about 18 MW are expected to be operational by
February 2007. An alternative to additional thermal generation based in Bamako, such as the
construction of larger thermal plants in Dakar or Nouakchott to help meet the deficit in Mali,
is also under consideration. Technical discussions are on-going between the countries
involved to confirm the feasibility of this alternative. It is expected that the generation
production mix will shift fiom a predominantly low cost hydro-based generation to a more
expensive thermal generation, with straightforward consequences for the tariff levels that are
consistent with the sector's financial viability, prior to commissioning of the interconnection
with neighboring countries.

5.102 Access expansion in rural and peri-urban areas to support productive activities:
Another important challenge is to successfully implement on-going decentralized energy
access expansionprograms. While the perimeter of EDM SA covers about 100 localities, the
majority of the country still remains in the dark (the electricity access rate in rural and peri-
urban areas is about 2 percent). This calls for a spectrum of innovative service delivery
mechanisms, with the active participation of communities, NGOs, and the private sector.
Progress made in the last two years should be sustained not only to ensure an increasing
number of connections but also to ensure that energy services are impacting the
competitiveness of small and medium size enterprises and social programs, thus facilitating
the achievement of the Millennium Development Goals. The on-going Bank-financed
Household Energy and Universal Access Project presented to the Board in 2003, is producing
some encouraging results on the grounds but the observed low level of access in rural areas
requires a massive scaling up effort for energy services to contribute significantly to rural
economictransformation.

5.103 Accelerate interconnections with neighboring countries: Interconnection with
neighboring countries is the least-cost option to availability of low-cost generated power for
Mali. In the medium to longer term, Mali needs to complete works enabling interconnection
with Cbte d71voire, Burkina Faso and Ghana. The GOM is currently working on the
interconnection with C8te D'Ivoire taking into account constraints imposed by the continued
political crisis in Cbte D'Ivoire. Reinforcement of the transmission line between Laboa in
Northern Cbte D'Ivoire and Ferkessedougou is needed to ensure a 200 MW capacity
availability to Mali. Discussions are also on-going between the Volta River Authority

(Ghana), Sociktk Nationale d'electricitk du Burkina (Burkina Faso), EDM SA and respective
ministries of these countries on the interconnection project Han (Ghana)-Bobo Dioulasso
(Burkina Faso)-Sikasso (Mali). The European Investment Bank has accepted in December
2006 to provide financing of the feasibility, line route, and environmental and social impact
assessment studies of this interconnection project. The GOM is strongly encouraged to move
quickly to materialize the construction of the transmission lines either with C6te D'Ivoire
andlorwith Burkina Faso to benefit from low-cost generated electricity.

Government action and medium-term obiectives

5.104 The GOM's energy sector policy letter of 1999 stated the following as main sector
objectives: (i) the provision of access to energy to the majority of the population at an
affordable cost; (ii) the development of all available national energy sources (hydro, solar,
wind, and biomass); (iii) the liberalizationof the energy sectorby encouragingparticipationof
private operators and initiatives of decentralized municipalities;and, (iv) the strengtheningof
institutions in charge of strategy development and monitoring of sector activities. Overall, the
policy letter called for improved sectorefficiency, the need to withdraw the public sector from
all operational responsibilities in the sector, and to extend service coverage.

5.105 The Government,by signingthe concession contracts on water and electricityin 2000,
made an important step towards the opening of these sectors to competition with the
expectation of mobilizing private sector financing. In addition, to ensure transparency and
efficiency in the management of the two sectors, including the application of legal and
regulatory rules, an independent water and electricity regulatory agencywas created by law in
2000 and became operational in 2001.

5.106 Since mid-2002, the GOM noted that EDM SA's investments were lagging. It has
been expected that EDM SA would make substantial investments in the water and electricity
sectors not only to ensure an increased reliability and efficiency of the systems, but also to
expand access to populations in Bamako and main cities of the country within the perimeters
of the concessions. SAURIIPS pointed to difficulties in mobilizing financial resources on the
commercial market and encouraged the GOM to consider concessional loans to help finance
investments in the sectors. SAUR/IPS also argued that the GOM tariff policy was confusing
and was not givingthe right signalsto potential investors.The GOM disagreed and stated that
it was providing compensations to cover the tariff shortfall and that SAUR/JPS was not
hlfilling the contractual obligations related to investments. Considerable time was spent by
parties to the contracts in agreeing on rules and data governing tariff levels and
compensations. On October 14, 2005, the GOM and SAUR International agreed on an
amicableseparation, SAUR International sold its sharesto IPS which in return sold them back
to the State of Mali. In order words, the State of Mali became the majority shareholder in
EDM SA with 66 percent of the sharesand 34 percent to IPS.

5.107 The sector reform experience over the last five years indicates that more work is
needed to strengthen the legal and regulatory environment to foster a lasting competitive
business environment attractive to private investors and operators. So far, Mali was not
successfulin putting in place a transparent and predictable framework, in which the regulatory
process is based around consensual rules, and in which the operator has adequateperformance
incentives and is held accountable for the achievement of clearly defined and measured

performance targets. Technical and financial reporting produced by EDM SA can also be
improved. For instance, it does not provide enough transparency with regard to the costs of the
different activities. Also, the sector regulator has not elaborated a clear and coherent
regulatory doctrine. The regulator has still to provide indicationsregarding the basis on which
tariffs will be established going forward. In the short term, the GOM is implementing an
action plan to improve EDM SA's financial and operational performance during a three year
transitional period (2006-2008)before launchingthe search for another majority private sector
shareholder. This plan also includes activities aimed at rehabilitating existing production
units, strengtheningtransmission and distribution networks, and implementinga demand-side
management program. First results of the implementation of this plan indicate cost efficiency
gains. EDM SA is paying more attention to internal and external fraud, savings were made on
expatriate salary budgets, and demand side management initiatives are being introduced in
public administrationbuildings.

5.108 On access expansion in rural and pen-urban areas, the GOM is working with the Bank
since 2002, and with the KfW, and the Swedish Cooperation since 2004 through AMADER
(Agence Malienne pour I'Energie Domestique et I'Electrification Rurale) to explore
decentralized energy services delivery schemes in order to accelerate the provision of energy
services to the poor. Interventions are articulated around three main areas: (i) capacity
building and institutional strengthening of AMADER, the Regulatory Commission, the
National Directorate of Energy, the National Directorate for the Conservation of Nature to
enhance their capabilities in mobilizing and regulating private operators; (ii) promotion of
private sector-based decentralized energy services delivery schemes as the most cost-effective
way to increase rural access to basic electricity services; and (iii) promotion of community-
based woodland management and inter-fuel substitution initiatives. In the medium term, the
GOM expects to attract more donors to help on access expansion initiatives based on results
of the on-goingprograms.

PRSC-1 actions supporting the Government'smedium-termprogram

5.109 The Bank through PRSC-1 to PRSC-3, intends to help the GOM tackle energy sector
issues, particularly the restructuring of EDM SA to minimize negative impacts of power
shortages on growth and competitiveness. A financially viable and operationally efficient
EDM SA will contribute to reduce sector subsidies and thereby improve macro and fiscal
balances. PRSC-1 actions are based on the GOM-adopted action plan for EDM SA
restructuring. They are basically centered on accelerating technical studies on institutional
arrangements,tariffs, least-cost investment plan, and audits of EDM SA. It is expected that all
technical studies will be completed in the period of PRSC-2 and that findings and
recommendations will provide a sound technical basis for decision making on sector
developmentin the period of PRSC-3.

Expected benefits of the PRSC-1 actions

5.110 Actions in the period of PRSC-1 to PRSC-3 related to the energy sector are expected
to help the GOM address poor performance issues of EDM SA and also to help create sector
benchmarks towards an improved legal and regulatory environment. In the short term, these
actions will contribute to put EDM SA on the path to better financial and operational

performance, that are strong prerequisites to fully capitalize on medium term and long term
interconnectioninvestments.

2E. CottonSector

Key issues and challen~es

5.111 Even though cotton is not the most important crop in value terms grown in Mali,
cotton has historically been Mali's most important cash and export crop, providing relatively
good livelihoods for thousands of fanners. Cotton production in Mali has expanded
remarkably in the years following the devaluation of the CFA Franc in 1994, from 293,021
tons of seed cotton in 1994-95to 522,903 tons in 1998-99.In 2006, production of seed cotton
is expected to be around 600,000 tons and about 3 millions people are living directly or
indirectly from cotton cultivation and associated businesses. Cotton cultivation enabled
producers in the south of Mali to become the most equipped of the country's producers, to get
regular input supplyand became the biggest producers of cerealsin the country.

5.112 The cotton sector is critical to Mali's macroeconomic stability-in     1997-98,the sector
accounted for half of export revenue, 9 percent of GDP, and 6 percent of government tax
revenue; however, cotton exports were only 21.4 percent of total export revenue in 2001-
2003. This decrease in economic impact of the cotton sector began in 1999. Since then, the
CMDT, the state cotton company, has been confronted with a severe financial crisis, due
partly to the drop in world prices for lint in 1998,but also to serious financial and technical
mismanagement. In addition, cotton sector growth was due exclusively to cultivated area
expansion rather than productivity growth and did not result in poverty reduction.
Nonetheless, Mali's cotton regions are currently among the poorest in the country. The 2005
national poverty assessment reveals that poverty remains high and unchanged in the cotton
zone, while it has decreased in Skgou, Mali's main irrigated rice production region.

5.113 In August 2000, Mali's Prime Minister requested the Bank's assistanceto help design
and implement a comprehensive restructuring program for this key sector of the country's
economy. A national forum on cotton was organized in April 2001 with the participation of
farmers' representatives, to help restore the confidence of stakeholders in the sector's future
development. The Mali Government acknowledged at that time that the existing model-a
monopsony entrusted to CMDT, a parastatal company-had              clearly shown its limits and
needed to be replaced by a new sector model which entailed divestment from the state,
introduction of competition and greater involvementof the private sector.

5.114 On the basis of various studies and technical discussions, a strategy to reform the
sector and progressively transition to a liberalized cotton sector was designed and agreed upon
with government teams, farmers' representatives and other partners over a two and a half year
period. The objectivesof the reforms include: (i) the containment and reduction of production
costs, in order to align ex-factory prices with the price of fiber on the world market; (ii) an
improvement in crop yields by strengthening extension services to cotton producers,
implementing soil fertility restoration techniques and observance of the different technical
schedules; (iii) the promotion of grassroots organizations, most notably through the creation
of cotton producer associations;(iv) a strengtheningof the role of the private sector,producers
and decentralized communities in achieving the objectivesof the cotton sector; (v) an increase

in the sector's contribution to the national economy; (vi) a contribution to poverty reduction
and the improvement of revenues and the population's quality of life; and finally (vii) price
determination in the sectorbased on freenegotiation among actors.

5.115 In July 2004 however, the President of Mali decided to postpone implementation of
the reforms. In November 2005, at the request of the Prime Minister, the Bank sent a joint
mission to Mali to resume the dialogue on the cotton sector and boost the reform process.
Farmers' representatives and main donors supporting the sector hlly participated in the
mission. The main outcome of the mission was: (i) confirmation by the Government of its
commitment to engage in a comprehensive sector reform by 2008; (ii) agreement on a revised
timetable for the comprehensive cotton sector reform, prepared by the Government in
participatory manner; (iii) selection of a set of benchmarks to monitor implementation of the
timetable; (iv) establishment of a steering committee including farmers and donors
representatives, to meet regularlyto monitor implementation of the timetable.

5.116 The Bank and the core group of partners (AFD, Dutch and EU) committed to support
the revised timetable for the cotton sector reforms through ongoing projects and budgetary
support. Annex 8 discusses Mali's cotton sector development and future challenges. Issues
currentlyfaced by the sectorare summarized as follows:

5.117 Inequitable distribution of cotton revenues. Cotton sector development was led by
CMDT, a parastatal company which is vertically integrated and benefits from a monopsony
for purchasing seed cotton and fi-oma de facto monopoly on the sale of cotton inputs. Despite
its obvious success in developing the sector, Mali's model of a vertically integrated
monopsony has repeatedly shown its risks and limitations. The CMDT has had to be bailed
out of severe financial crises in 1986, 1991, 2000, and 2004. The company's deficit in 2000
was close to US$100 million, and was around US$78 million in 2004. Moreover, the
monopsony system has led in the past to indirect taxation of the producers when world prices
were high, as in the mid-1990s, and has passed on to the producers most of the risk from price
and exchange rate fluctuations. From 1994 to 1998, farmers only received on average 37
percent of the export price, which is very low by any standards. And during the 2000-01
season when world prices were low, Malian fanners boycotted cotton cultivation in protest
about the low prices they were offeredby the CMDT for their cotton output.

5.118 In response, the Government set up a new price mechanism to correct the bias against
producers by linking the producer price to international market conditions, and delinking
farmers' revenues fi-om CMDT management performance. The protocol governing the new
price setting mechanism was signed by the Government, farmers and the CMDT in January
2005, to be in effect until privatisation of the CMDT in 2008. A particular feature of the new
mechanism is its objective of maximizing farmers' revenues and minimising budget risks
through: (i) the requirement that the initial price (set in April) at the beginning of the crop
season be in the range of CFAF 160-175 per kilogram, and that the price be revised in August
if world prices fall significantly; and (ii) a pre-determined distribution of the gross sector
income between producers (60%) and the CMDT (40%) (Annex 9 presents findings of the
analysis of the impact of producer price changes). During the post-privatization period the
cotton interprofession agency, to be created to include farmersand the new private companies,
will be in charge of taking decisions on the price settingmechanism.

5.119 The weak performance of CMDT: Due to the fall in world cotton prices and weak
management of CMDT, the cotton sector experienced a continuous financial crisis from 1999
to 2001. The Government thus decided to undertake a vast reform of the sector aiming at
improving the sector's competitiveness and performance. After a first unsuccessful attempt to
privatize a part of the CMDT in 2003, the Government adopted a revised plan in November
2005 to restructure cotton sector by 2008. Although the PRSC-1 pre-evaluationmission rated
the implementation of the reforms as satisfactory, a new issue was identified. The companyis
again experiencing a new financial crisis in 2005-06 due both to external shocks and a
continuing weak financial and technical management. Both the CMDT privatization
operational plan (Schema operational de privatization de la CMDT) and the 2005-06 crop
season outcome report (Rapport bilan de la campagne 2005-2006) stressed that the CMDT's
ginning factories are deteriorating due to a lack of regular and adequate maintenance; the
company is facing a huge deficit amounting to CFAF 43 billion for 2005 and CFAF 11billion
for the 2005-06 crop season; the CMDT's treasury gap is up to CFAF 86 billion; and the
CMDT's capital assets decreased to a minus CFAF 33.79 billion compared with a social
capital of CFAF 32.5 billion in 2005.

5.120 According to OHADA rules, a company with such a performance is bankrupt and
should be liquidated or recapitalized within two years. The Government decided to
recapitalize CMDT after the 2004 lossesby increasingits share in CMDT by 15percent. The
French shareholderDAGRIS did not participate in the recapitalizationand agreed to reduce its
share in the company from 40% to 25%. But the problem still remains with the new 2005
deficit. It is therefore urgent that this issue be adequately addressed through safeguard
measures in order to maintain an adequate and attractive company to investors.

5.121 InsufJcient involvement of farmers in the sector's decision making: Farmers are
very weakly organized and are currently represented in the cotton dialogue by the unions. A
process to structure the fanner organizations (FOs) into cooperatives, and a communication
campaign to get them hlly informed on the reforms, are being implemented, supported
through two donor projects: the Bank's PASAOP and French development agency's PASE.
The Government has decided to allocate 20 percent of the privatized companies' shares to
farmers through the cooperatives. The structuringof farmers organization is thus an important
step in the privatization process. The hture "Federation Nationale des Cooperatives de
Cotton" (an umbrella of the producer organizations) will be the legal representative of
producer organizations.

5.122 Stagnant cotton productivity: To date, Mali has increased cotton production by
expanding the amount of land under cultivation. At farm level, productivity and yields have
been declining for 15 years. However, it is increasingly important for Mali to focus on
increasing yields and improving cotton qualityto maximize the sector's competitiveness and
profitability. Many of Mali's competitors are using biotech cotton (Burkina in West Africa is
developing new biotech varieties) but Mali has not yet begun testing of geneticallymodified
organisms (GMO). Before the move to GMO cotton in Mali, however, there is an urgency for
the country to design a strategy for increasing productivity, including the adoption of
improved soil and water management practices, de-linking of credit provision and area
cropped, emphasis on quality-basedprices for cotton purchases from farmers, and creating the
conditions to enable availability of credit for other crops so as to improve the optimality of
fanners' cropping decisions.

Governmentaction and medium-term oblectives

5,123 Theprice mechanism has so far been implemented successfully for two crop seasons,
resulting in additional price payments to the farmers. This mechanism should be kept till the
privatisation of CMDT and its management transferred to the "inter-profession'' in the post-
privatisation period. Farmers are setting up a price stabilisation fund with the support of the
EU, which will be operational during the 2006-07 crop season and will strengthen the price
mechanism by securingprices paid to farmers.

5.124 Government adopted aprivatization scheme whereby the CMDT will be divided in 4
blocks to be privatized (Council of Ministers of March 9, 2006) and is processing the
recruitment of the privatization adviser (investment Bank) whose contract is expected to be
signedby February 2007. Due to the importance of the production and its distributionover the
south and west of the country, it was decided to split the company in 4 balanced zones, as
specified in the privatization operational plan adopted by the Government in October 2006.
The capital of the private companies will be shared by private owners (61%), producers
(20%), State (17%) and CMDT workers (2%).

5.125 Government undertook a program to empower farmer organizations. In this
framework, the Government is implementing a comprehensive capacity building and
communication program to support the establishment and progressive federation of farmer
organizations, and the building of their capacities to participate in and benefit from the
privatization process. Almost all village associations have been transformed into cooperatives
and federated at the commune level. The last step is the establishment of the national
federation of producer organizations. The Government has committed to finalize the
establishmentof federationproducer organizationsby January 2007.

5.126 To mitigate the effect of CMDT weak management, the Government decided to
recapitalize CMDT in 2005 to comply with the OHADA regulation, and provided CMDT
with a loan amounting at FCFA 28 millions from the national budget, disbursed in 2005
(CFAF 20 million) and 2006 (FCFA 8 million). In addition to these measures, the
Government committed to undertake close surveillance of CMDT operations through the
organization of monthly meetings of the treasury committee set up to advise CMDT on
treasurymanagement.

5.127 To boost cotton productivity, CMDT organized a stakeholder workshop in August
2006 that resulted in the production of an action plan to improve seed cotton productivity and
quality. Main actions in the plan include a manure production campaign, training of farmers
on various technologies (cotton quality improvement techniques, integrated pest
management), and timely provision of inputs. A steeringcommittee composed of farmers and
CMDT representatives was set up to meet monthly to monitor implementation of the action
plan. Although the action plan contains too many activities and lacks focus, it is a good step
toward addressing productivity issues. The Government committed to closely follow up
implementation of the productivity improvement action plan designed by CMDT and cotton
farmers. In addition, the Government developed a strategic development framework ("Cadre
Stratkgique de Developpement du Secteur Coton") which addresses the need to boost cotton
sectorproductivity. This framework is under discussion between Government and the donors.

PRSC-1 actions supportinnthe Government's medium-termpromam

5.128 There is a rationale for the Bank to continue support to the Government to implement
cotton sector reforms as well as mitigation and safeguardmeasures to ensure that the company
will continue to operate and improve its performance until the privatization of CMDT occurs
in 2008. After a thorough analysis of the sector situation, it was agreed with Government and
the main donors to focus PRSC-1 on the following selected measures for 2006, all of which
have alreadybeen completed:

    o Continuethe implementation of the cottonproducer price mechanism;
    o Definition ofthe CMDT zonesto be privatized;
    o Adoption (bythe Council of Ministers) of the final privatizationoperationplan;
    o Launch the call forbids to recruit the privatization advisor(investmentbank).


Expected Benefits of the PRSC-1 Actions

5.129 Expected results for PRSC-1 include: (i) a price mechanism that guarantee 60 percent
of export price to farmers, (ii) adoption of the privatization law by the Government; (iii)
improvement of CMDT treasury management; and (iv) finalization of the restructuring of
cooperatives.

5.130 For the three-year PRSC series, expected results include: (i) equitable and adequate
revenue to farmers; (ii) a privatized and revitalized, dynamic cotton sector managed with the
participation of farmers; (iii) significantly increased production and yields; (iv) improved
supply chains for both input and output and increased ability to provide value-added services
(ginning); and (v) decreasedpoverty in the cottonzone.

2F. Officedu Niger Irrigation Zone

Key issues and challenges

5.131 The Niger River Inigation Office (Ofice du Niger -ON) manages the development of
irrigable lands on the left bank of the Niger river, east of the Markala dam which is located
downstream of S6gou. Completionof the canals and the first land developmentswere in 1935,
and the irrigated areas increased gradually to reach 53,000 hectares in the 1960s. Since then,
expansion of the canal network, and thus irrigated areas, has stagnated. Initial production
focused on cotton and rice, with rice becoming the dominant crop as cotton was abandoned
for various reasons. Following a period of stagnation of rice production, there was an
extended production boom from 1994 due to a rehabilitation of canals, on-farm technical
reforms and changes in fertiliseruse, all resulting from significant institutional reforms within
the Office and policy reforms at the national level (e.g., rice market liberalisation,tax reforms,
and devaluation of the CFA franc). This spectacular expansion attracted large numbers of
immigrants who, with no available developed lands in the area covered by the Office, started
to cultivate lands not initially intended for that purpose, thereby generating some problems
