still being faced today. Management of the ON is governed by performance contracts agreed
with the farmers and the Government-the         first dates back to 1996-1998, and the current
fourth contract covers the 2005-07 period. The ON today oversees management of 82,000
hectares of fully-controlled irrigation areas, out of a potential of at least 1million hectares.

5.132 Key issues currently faced by ON include: (i) the need to strengthen management of
the ON, (ii) the slow development of the ON's irrigationpotential, (iii) the lack of a long term
vision and strategy for sustainable development of the ON zone, and (iv) the focus of ON
intervention on rice and the limited introduction of high value crops to improve profitability
of the imgated land.

5.133 Strenghening the ON's management. The Office du Niger underwent some
institutional reforms during the 1990s,which placed it among the better-performing irrigated
commands in Sub-Saharan Africa and improved rice productivity and profitability. Yields
were improved from 2 tons per hectare (ha) before the reforms, to 6 tons per ha in 2004.
Nevertheless there is a need to further focus the ON in water management and maintenance of
the dam and main structures, and empower producer organizations to take charge of the
maintenance of secondary and tertiary canals as well as of extension, research and input
supply functions.

5.134 Accelerating the irrigation land development in the ON. The potential of imgation
land in ON area is estimated at 1 million hectares, of which only about 82,000 hectares are
currently developed. The development of new imgated lands in the zone is extremely slow,
despite the obvious and proven benefits of mitigating risks of climate variability. The major
challenge is establishing a process to accelerate imgation land development, drawing on
private as well as public financing. Under the National Rural Infrastructure Program (PNIR),
the Bank supported the Government to explore new irrigation funding mechanisms,
specifically, approaches to attract private sector hnding for more rapid expansion of irrigated
areas in the Officedu Niger zone.

5.135 Designing a vision and strategyfor sustainable development of ON zone. The CEM
acknowledges the impact of rice development in the ON in reducing poverty and increasing
agricultural growth. But the ON is considered an island of prosperity that is totally
disconnected from the development of the administrative regions covered by ON intervention
(the ON zone). In addition,there is no coherent framework that would maximize the impact of
Donors' intervention in the ON zone. Currently, the main instrument that governs ON
development is the 3-years performance contract signed between the Government, the ON,
and the producers. In order to obtain a sustainable impact in poverty reduction and growth,
there is a need to integrate ON development in a broader development of the ON in relation
with Mali's decentralization policy and taking into account the overall potential of these
regions. For the moment there is no medium term and long term vision and strategy. There is
a need to develop such a strategic vision, shared by all stakeholders including the state, local
Government and farmers, so as to ensure a greater impact of Government and donors
financing. The Ministry of Agriculture is preparing a comprehensive master plan for the
development of the ON zone aimed at resolving this issue.

5.136 Developing incentivesfor commercial agriculture in ON zone. The ON put a lot of
emphasis on the development of rice production once the cotton program that was the initial
focus of the ON was abandoned. Emphasis was also put on smallholder farmers involved in
subsistence agriculture with no secured access to land. In order to improve the contribution of
the ON zone to agricultural growth, there is a need to encourage commercial f m i n g with the
introduction of high value crops and a secure land tenure system.

    Government action and medium-term obiectives

    5.137 The Government is undertaking a comprehensive organizational audit of the Office du
   Niger,with a view to better rationalizemanagement, enhance producer capacitiesand build a
   balanced relationship between the ON and producers in water management. The first draft of
   the audit was issued in November 2006, and commented on by all stakeholderson December
    14, 2006. The final version containing an action plan will be submitted to the ON Board of
    Governors for adoption in April 2007.

    5.138 Under the PNIR,the Government is piloting two financing mechanisms to attract
    private sector financing of irrigation including: (i) an experimental test whereby small-scale
    fanners (3 ha each) and large-scale (more than 30 ha) commercial farmers who can access
    land title are settled in the Komouna zone, to co-finance the development of irrigation area;
    and (ii) a study to set up an irrigation funding mechanism whereby the private sector will get
    incentives to invest in the development of irrigated land. The Government has already
    developed the Koumouna perimeter (1115 ha) and selected 130 small-scale farmers to be
    settled there. The selection process for the large-scale farmers (30 ha) is underway. A
    complementary study is being conducted to strengthen the proposal of the irrigation private
    financing mechanism. The first draft of the proposal for the design of the fimding mechanism
    should be available early in 2007.

    5.139 With the participation of the main stakeholders, the Government prepared a
    comprehensive master plan for development of the ON zone that underlines the vision and
    strategy for development of the ON zone including the integration of decentralizationaspects.
    The plan addresses the main strategic issues not only for the irrigated zone covered by ON,
    but include also the main aspect of the comprehensive development of agriculture, livestock
    as well as local development. The master plan is a key instrument for aligning and
    harmonizing donor interventions in the ON. The final report is available, and what remains is
    the official adoptionof the masterplan and monitoring of its implementation.

    PRSC-1 actions supportingthe Government'smedium-term program

    5.140 The Bank is well positioned to continue to support ON reforms and rice sector
    development that are key for poverty reduction and growth in Mali. As public resources for
    irrigation land development are limited and decreasing, a combination of public and private
    financing is needed to accelerate irrigated land development and the realization of the huge
    potential of the ON. Therefore the Bank agreed with Government and main donors that the
    Government would focus on the following measures for the PRSC-I, all of which have
    alreadybeen completed:

        o Undertake a complementary study to strengthen the proposal for private irrigation
,           financing;

        o Makethe first draft of the ON audit available.

    Expected benefits of the PRSC-1 actions

    5.141 It is expected that these PRSC-1measures will lay the foundation for: (i) establishinga
    sustainable irrigation hnding mechanism which will attract private operators to invest in the

ON so as to increase irrigated land, introduce improved technologies, and diversify produce
demanded by national, sub-regional and international market; (ii) ensuring the transfer of
responsibility for water management, from the ON to water users, in line with the
rationalization of water fees to maintain of the canals; (iii) sustainably developing the ON
through harmonized donor interventions in line with Mali's decentralization policy; and (iv)
improvingyields, production, and producers' revenues in the ON zone.


Component3: Access to Basic Social Services

5.142 The PRSC-1 includes two components addressing access to quality basic social
services. The first component focuses on health and social development, while the second
focuses on rural water supply and sanitation. The operation doesn't include education since
the sector's issues are addressed under a recently approved Second Education Sector
Investment Program II (FY06; Credit number IDA-42220) and there are as yet no
complementarypolicy measures to be tackled in the context of the PRSC.


3A. Health and Social Development

Key issues and challenges

5.143 Following on the health sector-wide approach which Government started
implementing in the 1 9 9 0 ' ~progress in the health sector is noticeable, albeit slow. The
                                 ~
Government's health sector strategy has always emphasized access to basic social services as
a priority area. Access to health services has thus improved slightly with the number of
fbnctionalcommunity health centers increasing from 605 to 753 between 2002 and 2005, for a
target of 1109 centers. This has resulted in some noticeable improvements (see table page 2)
in many indicators, and other indicators are mostly going in the right direction.

5.144 Government has identified several issues that need to be addressed. The first pertains
to the low utilization of health services by the population. This is due to several factors such
as lack of drugs in public health centers as well as poor quality of care there. Operational
research has shown that if a person belongs to a mutuelle (a local insurance scheme), helshe
will utilize services more, mainly because the mutuelle can bargain for better serviceswith the
health center it contracts with. Thus, there's a push to ensure more people belong to this risk-
pooling mechanism. Data also shows that Malians, and especially poor Malians, seek care in
the non-state sector, which is not well regulated, It is thus important to harness the potential of
that sector and ensure it contributes to Mali's health sector objectives.

5.145 The second issue rests with the rapid population growth of Mali, which is the second
fastest growing in the world after that of Niger. This situation is due to a relative decrease in
mortality without a slowdown in fertility, as a woman in Mali has 6.8 births during her life
time. Such high fertility rate also has the consequence of high child mortality, due to the low
interval between births, and high maternal mortality rates. So, in spite of efforts to increase
access to health, the Governmenthas to invest more than the population growth rate if it wants
health indicators to improve.

5.146 The third issue is that of poor quality of care in hospitals. This is a complex issue
which needs to be dealt with at different levels, by motivating human resources, and by

improving working environment, for example. To that end, Government will implement a
new approach by signing performance-based contracts with the autonomous hospitals. This
new approach will be evaluated yearly.

5.147 Finally, as with other sectors, the decentralization reform has led to major changes in
the health sector. The new district administrations are now in charge of health administration
and new responsibilities are being given to them to provide technical supervision of health
centers. Close attention to the consequences of these governance changes is important, to
ensure that the Ministry of Health (MOH) has necessary resources to support implementation.

Government action and medium-term objectives

5.148 Government designed a ten year strategy(PRODESS) which it started implementing in
2000, through yearly actionplans agreed with donors and adopted by the Government.Donors
follow this strategy and finance the yearly action plans. The PRODESS objectives aim at
reaching the MDGs through into two components, each managed by a separate ministry
(Health and Social Development). The second phase of PRODESS (2006-lo), currently under
implementation, uses the MTEF to ensure that the annual action plans are appropriate and
funding adequate to reach the objectives. In this second half, more emphasis is placed on
consolidating certain activities which were successful in reaching the objectives during the
first phase, namely the immunization program, the construction of new health centers, the
planning and monitoring process. Now, there's a need to render all the health centers
operational (with proper human resources and equipment), to harness the existing private
sector, and to shoreup utilization of health services.

5.149 The apparent lack of human resources in the health sectoris due in great part to a poor
geographical distribution with high concentration in Bamako. The Government hires new
recruitson temporary contractsusing HlPC resources.

5.150 The MOH has developed a medical waste management plan during the preparation of
the MAP, and is implementing it. The World Health Organization and other donors are
helping Government to update this plan on a regular basis and in ensuring it is put into
practice.

5.151 Efforts made to date in the sector have translated into the following outcomes (see
table below); however, much more needs to be done to have an impact on maternal and child
mortality which remain some of the highest in the world. This high mortality is due in great
part to preventable diseases such as diarrhea, malaria, measles, malnutrition, birth related
deaths.


     Indicator                                                 2001   2003      2005
     Assisted deliveries (%)                                   27    42         53
     Percent of pregnant women who went to at least 1prenatal  47     59        75
     consultation
     Percent of childrenless than 1year old completely         29     79        91
     vaccinated

5.152 Regarding resource use, the budget execution rate in the 2005 action plan was around
95 percent. However, the rate of execution for external financing remains in general much
below that. This is due in great part to difficulty in mobilizing promised aid, and in the
sometimes complicated procedures of certain donors which slow down disbursements. The
shift to a budget support approach by the Bank (under the PRSC) and other donors will thus
help solve someof those problems.

5.153 In terms of health impact and outcome, it's acknowledged that Mali will not reach the
MDGs in 2015, except for the objective pertaining to vaccination rates. However, the trends
are in the right direction, indicators are improving, and the MOH is on its way to
implementing the equity measures it set for itself (dividing the country in three zones
according to poverty level, and trying to invest more resources in the poorer zones).

5.154 The MOH is also recognizing the importanceof the existingprivate health care sector
(there are more medical doctors in the private sector than in the public sector, not counting
those that practice in both sectors). The Government has thus started working on a contracting
policy and it developed some standard contractsto ease the process.

PRSC-1 actions supporting the Government's medium-term program

The PRSC addresses three key sector objectives: (i) to increase the supply and quality of
health services; (ii) to consider the health system as a whole (private and public); and (iii) to
increase utilizationof health services. Thesethree issue areasare discussed in turn below.

(i) To increasethe supplyand quality of health services:

5.155 Although the country might benefit from its rapid population growth in terms of
'demographic bonus', there's still unrnet need for birth spacing that remains to be covered.
Use of modern contraception has stagnated at 6 percent for the past 10 years. The MOH is
very aware of this situation and would like to lead an effort to ensure that the 30 percent of
women who have unrnet needs in terms of being able to plan their pregnancies are satisfied. In
order to increase contraceptive prevalence rate, the MOH will design a plan to strengthen
family planning service availability and outreach for the health centers. The effectiveness of
this program will be monitored by calculating the "couple-years of protection" based on data
from health structures. This indicator was recently introduced in the health informationsystem
and thus no baseline yet exists.

5.156 Increasing contraceptive prevalence rates will require investments in information and
communication effort as well as a better monitoring of family planning services provided by
the public sector. Other donors (in particular UNFPA and USAID) will need to work closely
with the MOH to ensure that coordinated effortsare implemented.

5.157 In order to improve quality of care in hospitals, Government started designing a
performance-based contracting mechanism with each hospital. This is a new venture which is
expected to have high impact if properly implemented. One of the first steps is to design
standard performance contracts,which will be done by December 2006.

5.158 The 2007 Plan of Action for each ministry (Health and Social Development) includes
about US$4 millions which were to be provided by an IDA project. Now that the project will
no longer take place and that its resources are folded into the PRSC, the resources should be
made available through the national budget. However, due to the timing of the change from
project to PRSC, the 2007 national Budget does not yet include the additional provision for
the two ministries. It is important that the two ministries be able to implement their 2007
action plans as designed, without a deficit due to the change in instrument used for financing
                                                                                               .
by the World Bank. It was thus agreed with Government that in 2007, once the PRSC
resources are disbursed, an amount at least equivalent to US$4 million will be provided for
the two ministries ($3 million for Health and $1 million for Social Development). The MOF
confirmed this arrangementthrough a letter to the Bank

5.159 Most health servicesare provided at the local level, in the regions. Each Region has its
own annual action plan to reach the MDGs. In 2006, CFAF 4.3 billion were given directlyto
the health regions (13 percent) excluding regional hospitals. However, if one takes into
account all the expenses made for the regions too, this percentage increases to 35 percent for
the regions, 12 percent for central hospitals, and 53 percent for central structures. It is
expected that the amount for the regions shouldnot fall below the current 35 percent.

(ii) To considerthe health system as a whole (private and public):

5.160 Considering that there are as many medical doctors working in the private sector as
those working in the public sector (data from the 'Ordre des Mbdecins'), and that a majority
of poor people bring their sick children (69 percent) to the non-state sector, the Government
has recently decided to work more closelywith the private sector.

5.161 This will be done by including private service providers in government-organized
training programs, by more contracting out of service provision, and by harnessing the
potential of local companies to address certain diseases among their workers, through
corporate responsibility. Contracting out health service provision to the private sector,
including NGOs, will allow not only the inclusion of health statistics from the private sector
into the public system, but will help the MOH ensure its own objectives are reached. In its
contracts with NGOs it will put the focus on increasing the number of properly assisted
deliveries. This indicatorshould contributeto decreasingmaternal and neo-natal deaths.

5.162 There's also a problem of ensuring that the country is well covered in terms of health
services. To do so a 'carte sanitaire' is being designed with previsions for the next 5 years.
Such an instrument will include also existingprivate clinics and offices.

(iii) To increase utilization of health services:

5.163 In order to increase utilization of services and to improve health care quality, the MOH
is taking several measures among which: working more closely with the private sector,
increasing the coverage of health mutuelles, and implementing a pilot with hospitals on
performance-basedcontracting.

5.164 Mutuelles, which pool funds from community members to cover a package of basic
health services provided at the health center level and for the transfer of patients, when

needed, to referral hospitals, have been piloted successfully over the last ten years. The
objective is to smooth the cost of health services for members, eliminating the hardship of
making payment for health services out of pocket. Mutuelles play an important role in
intermediatingbetween health centers,districthospitals, and the population. Evaluations show
that mutuelles are more effective when they have strong community participation in their
governance structures and make payments to the health centers on a per capita basis,
essentially transferring all risk to health centers. In 2005 and 2006, the focus was on building
administrative and management support and technical capacity, including training and
development of appropriate tools. In 2007, the GoM will increase rnutuelles coverage for the
poorer zones.

Expected benefits of the PRSC-1 actions

5.165 Because the World Bank has accompanied the MOH and the MDS since the 1990sin
its SWAP approach, the shift fiom financing through a project to a budget support will be
relatively easy. The main benefits will consist in (i) putting more focus on results than on the
process; (ii) decreasing the fiagrnentation of aid through harmonization of procedures aligned
on governmentprocedures.

5.166 The indicators chosen in the PRSC matrix are taken fiom those already selected by
Government and other donors (Sweden, Holland, Canada) in their MOU on targeted budget
support.They themselves identified their indicators from the list of indicators followedby the
MOH and MDS in the PRODESS. Such harmonization is important to ensure we all follow
the same indicators. Only one, that for hospital perfonnance contracts is not part of
PRODESS, simply because it's a new approach which Government wants to try and decided
on lately.

5.167 Each of the PRSC-1 actionswill provide the followingbenefits:

       By introducing performance contracts with the hospitals, the PRSC-1 would lay the
        foundation for improved service quality during the first PRSC series.
       By designing an expansion plan of the family planning program the PRSC-1 will help
       prepare for the rapid expansion of family planning services to take place during the
        subsequent PRSCs. Those services should be part of the package of essential activities
       which are to be offered in all health centers.
       By ensuring that adequate financing for the two ministries is secured, PRSC-1 will set
       the stage for the yearly discussions which will take place between those ministries and
       the MOF regarding the need to adequately finance their annual plans of actions to
       reach the objectives.
       By making sure that attention is paid to financing the Regions, PRSC-1 will help
       ensure more resources will reach health services at the periphery, where most of the
       poor population lives.
       The 'carte sanitaire' is a 'sine qua non' for designing the MTEF, and by ensuring it
       includes the private sector services, PRSC-1 will help ensure that the whole health
       system is considered, notjust the public sector.
       By ensuring that standard contracts with NGOs are designed, PRSC-1 will set the
       stage for rapid contracting of NGOs and will ensure that a focus is put in these
       contracts on improvingthe coverageof properly assisted births during the next PRSCs.

        By having a plan for a rapid expansion of health mutuals during PRSC-1, this sets the
        stage for such expansion which should happen in the subsequentPRSCs.


3B. Potable water supplyprogram (outsidethe EDM area)

Key issues and challenges

5.168 Safe drinking water, sanitation and good hygiene are fundamental to health, survival,
growth and development. Efforts to prevent death from diarrhea or to reduce the burden of
diseases such as ascaris, dracunculiasis, hookworm, schistosomiasis and trachoma, will be
unsuccessfU1 unless people have access to safe drinking water and basic sanitation. Lack of
basic sanitation indirectly inhibits the learning abilities of thousands of school-aged children
who are infested with intestinal worms transmitted through inadequate sanitationfacilitiesand
poor hygiene.

5.169 Water and sanitation are of prime importance for the development of Mali because of
their complementary role in preventing disease and reaching other Millennium Development
Goals (MDGs). Access to improved water supply and sanitation, accompanied by effective
hygiene education, can be effective in improving both health and productivity, improving the
quality of life and providing increased opportunitiesfor education and employment, especially
for women and girls. According to the UNICEFIWHO Joint Monitoring Program 2004 report
(JMP), 36 percent of the rural population have access to potable water and 78 percent in urban
centers and for sanitation, the access rate to any type of improved sanitation facility is
estimated at 39 percent in rural areas and at 59 percent in urban area. Clearly the challenges
are in rural areas where most of the poor live and where access rates to safe water and basic
sanitationare lower than in urban centers.

5.170 Meetingthe MDG target, i.e. providing 75 percent of the population with safe drinking
water by 2015, represents a major challenge for Mali. To reach the MDG's, about 5 million
additional people would have to benefit from access to safe water and 7 million to sanitation,
and current execution capacity and financing support (that of the 1990-2004 period) would
have to be multiplied by 2.5 for safe drinkingwater and by more than 3 for sanitation.Clearly,
the current multi-donor project approach characterized by unpredictable and irregular flows of
funds, high dependency on external funding, use of various strategies and procedures, and
high transaction costs, cannot respond to the new challenges. This calls for a new approach
and a new sector-wide rural water supply and sanitation (RWSS) strategy for Mali to enable
scaling up of investment to enable meeting the MDGs. To do so, the RWSS sector will need
to seriously: (i) improve sector planning and coordination; (ii) accelerate the RWSS
investrnentfrehabilitation program activities and the mobilization of financial resources to
enable the construction of 1,250 water points a year compared to an average of 450 over the
past years. It will also require taking action to prevent current and future infrastructurefalling
into disrepair, as a result of insufficient cost-recovery,poor operation and maintenance and an
overall lack of soundmanagementpractices.

Government actions and medium-term obiectives

5.171 Since 2002, a series of actions were taken by the Government of Mali to improve the
legal, institutional and policy framework in the rural water supply and sanitation sector. First,

a new water law was adopted. Second, the responsibility for implementing the water and
sanitation program was conferred on the communes, in line with Mali's decentralization
policy. Third, the Direction Nationale de I'Hydraulzque (DNH) decided in 2004 to shiR from
a multi-donor project approach toward a sector-wide approach (SWAP) based on a national
program, to enable integrating planning activities in the sector, harmonizing donor support,
and scaling up of investments to reach the MDGs.

5.172 Despite the early decision to shift to a sector-wide approach, the RWSS SWAP
process has onlyjust commenced in 2006, and only a few of the required building blocks are
currently in place. First, an exhaustive water point inventory was carried out in 2002-2003,
and providing the sector with an updated and useful baseline which estimated that 64 percent
of the rural population have access to potable water. This data is quite different from the JMP
report mentioned above; nonetheless, it is the one which has been retained by the sector
authoritiesand therefore included in the PRSC matrix. Second, a National Program for Water
Supply was prepared and validated by the Government, then presented to the donor
community in December 2004. An estimate of about US$60 million per year would be
necessary to meet the MDGs for water supply, compared with US$25 million disbursed
annually in past years. Third, a coordination group between sector authorities and the donors
has been established. Finally, in June 2006, the DNH contracted consultantsto elaboratetheir
first medium term expenditure framework (MTEF) covering the period 2007-2009.
Unfortunately, the exercise could not be completed early enough to have any impact on 2007
budgetary allocation for the DNH.

5.173 In the medium term, a key Government priority is to translate the national water and
sanitationprogram into action.Key objectivesof the program are to: (i) provide all villages as
a priority with at least one potable water point (currently about 2000 villages have no potable
water point); (ii) implement the cost recovery policy for operation and maintenance so as to
reduce the share of non-functioning structures+urrently        30 percent of the 25,000 water
points (hand pumps, modem wells, and stand pipes) are broken); (iii) support the
decentralization policy and assist local governments to fully plan, execute and manage their
water supply systems; (iv) introduce innovative ways of managing water supply systems and
in particular encourage local public-private partnerships (PPP) between the communesand the
local private sector to manage the piped water systems; (v) fully integrate hygiene and
sanitation promotion activitiesinto water supplyprograms in orderto maximize the impact on
public health status; and finally (vi) create the environmentto facilitate the use and integrated
management of water, land and other related resources, in order to maximize the social and
economic benefits of clean water without compromising the sustainability of the critical
ecosystems. Another key activity planned for 2007 is the updating of the water supply sector
strategy to incorporate the decentralization policy, private sector participation, water resource
management,and genderissues, as well as integrating it with the sanitation strategy.

PRSC-1 actions supportingthe Government's medium-tern program

5.174 The Bank fully supports the shift toward the sector-wide approach for the RWSS
sector as decided by the Government in 2004. The SWAPis considered the best way to pursue
hdarnental policy reform, improve predictability of funding, stimulate donor harmonization
and alignment with country systems, maximize the impact of both government and external
finance, and scale up sustainableRWSS servicedelivery.

5.175 The PRSC series' policy measures focus on reforms for improving budget planning
and monitoring as well as donor harmonization, to ultimately increase sector absorptive
capacity. More specifically, the preparation of medium term expendituresEramework (MTEF)
of the Direction Nationale de 1'Hydraulique and of the Direction Nationale de
1 'Assainissementet du Contrde des Pollutions et Nuisances (DNACPN) and annual sector
reviews will serve as a basis for the move towards the SWAP and provide impetus to the
donor coordination and harmonization process by facilitating dialogue and providing a unified
reference framework for strategic and operational planning and monitoring of all rural water
supply,water resources management and sanitationpromotion activities. The World Bank,the
German Cooperation GTZ and KFW, the Swedish Cooperation (ASDI), the Danish aid
agency DANIDA and the French Development Bank (MD) have agreed to provide financing
and consultancy services to assist DNH and DNACPN to: (i) prepare the 2006 program
execution report (ii) elaborate the 2008-2010 MTEF; and (iii) organize the RWSS sector's
joint Govemmentldonor annual review. In addition, the authorities will update the texts
defining the mandate of DNH to include planning and monitoring mission of the water sector
program.

5.176 The PRSC also supports implementation of critical and specific reforms aiming at
increasing the functionalityand the sustainability of existing and future potable water supply
infrastructure, and the impact on health status of RWSS investmentsthrough implementation
of the new water points maintenance strategy, including the rehabilitation of nonfunctioning
water points and the promotion of publiclprivate partnerships (PPP) for improving the
management of piped water systems.

5.177 The World Bank will lead the preparation of a public expenditure review (PER) in the
RWSS sector in 2007. The main objective is to gain a better understanding of the financial
framework in which RWSS investments are made in Mali. The PER will ultimately help
sharpen the RWSS reform content of future PRSCs. The participatory process to be used to
prepare the PER and disseminate its findings, will help strengthen the Bank's policy dialogue
with the Government and other donors.

Expectedbenefits of the PRSC-1 actions

5.178 The PRSC series will contribute to improve RWSS sector capacity for planning and
monitoring, foster donor dialogue with the DNH and DNACPN, and provide an overall
framework for implementing and monitoring the National Water and Sanitation Program
(NWSP). These improvements will strengthen the foundation for improved implementationof
the sector program, thereby improving the prospects for achieving the objectives outlined in
the NWSP. Key medium term targets of the NWSP, which are adopted as PRSC benchmarks,
are the following: (i) access to safe water in rural areas increases from 64 percent in 2004 to
70 percent of the population in 2009; (ii) hnctioning rate of water points increases from 70 in
2004 to at least 80 percent in 2009; and (iii) at least 30 public private partnerships for
managingpiped water systemsin 2009.

                          6. OPERATION IMPLEMENTATION


                                POVERTY   AND SOCIALIMPACTS


6.1    The poverty and social impacts of the PRSC-1 are expected to be favorable
overall. Many of the measures have an indirect poverty and social impactthrough creatingthe
conditions for improved governance and provision of public services (i.e., macroeconomic
and public finance management), improved quality of infrastructure services (i.e., transport
road maintenance, irrigation reforms), improved health of and access to financial sector
services (i.e., financial sector reforms), and lower cost of goods and services (i.e., through
improved transport road maintenance and transit facilitation). The elimination of the export-
related "expertise tax" on handicrafts would benefit the poor since handicrafts are a major
source of income for large numbers of the poor. Handicraft activities are also the main source
of diversification outside agriculture in the rural areas, thereby translating into economic
opportunitiesfor the poorest Malians. As regards the cotton sector, the findings of the poverty
and social impact analysis (PSLA) of the cotton producer price reform, suggest a much less
adverse impact on fanners of the drop in producer prices than initially feared (Annex 9).


                      IMPLEMENTATION,MONITORING EVALUATION
                                                       AND


6.2    The Ministry of Economy and Finance is the main interlocutor of this operation,
and is responsible for overall coordination of supervision and monitoring of program
implementation, led by the Secretary General of the Ministry of Economy and Finance
(MEF) and drawing on MEF thematic technical advisors who liaise with individuals
designated as focal points in the departmentdagencies involved in the PRSC program. The
participating ministries, departments and agencies will furnish relevant information and
documentation on implementation and monitoring of their respective programs to MEF,
which will overseeprogress in achievingprogram objectives.




6.3    Mali's fiduciary framework is adequate overall to receive the proceeds of the
credit, given that the Government has adopted a public finance management (PFM)
action plan based on the last CFAA (2002) and CPAR update (2005), and the plan
addresses the main risk highlightedin the assessments.Implementation of the PFM action
plan (the PAGAM) is supported under the first component of the PRSC-1, building on
achievements made under the SAC III,SAC IV and EPPFMC operations. In addition, the
Government is establishing baseline indicators values through a Public ExpenditureFiduciary
Assessment (PEFA), to be able to monitor progress on public finance management over time.
This will form the basis for updating the CFAA in the planned Public Expenditure
Management and Fiduciary Assessment Review, which will feed into the policy matrix for
PRSC-2 and PRSC-3. At this stage, the preliminary PEFA indicatesthat: (i) budget credibility
is satisfactory, except for arrears management; (ii) budget comprehensiveness and
transparency are rated to be average, but budget information is weak; (iii) budgeting is based
on sectoral policies and pluriannual policies; (iv) predictability and internal controls have
some weaknesses, notably on procurement and general internal control; (v) accounting and

reporting, as well as external scrutiny and audits, are reported to be fairly weak. The
accounting and reporting weaknesses are addressed in the actions outlined in the PRSC-1
policy matrix. As regards the health sector financial management (FM), an FM assessment in
February 2006 found that the Health Ministry financial administration department was
appropriately qualified, experienced and capable of directing and guiding the financial
management operations.


                                DISBURSEMENT AUDITING
                                                 AND


6.4     The proposed credit will follow the Bank's disbursement procedures for
development policy credits. The untied budget support will be disbursed against satisfactory
implementation of the development policy program; it is not tied to any specific purchases,
and no procurement requirements will be needed. Once the Credit is approved by the Board
and becomes effective, the proceeds of the Credit will be deposited by IDA in an account
designated by the Borrower at the Central Bank of West African States (BCEAO) in Bamako
at the request of the Borrower. The Borrower shall ensure that upon the deposit of the IDA
tranche into said account, an equivalent amount is credited in the Borrower's budget
management system. If the proceeds of the Credit are used for ineligible purposes as defined
in the Financing Agreement, IDA will require the Borrower to promptly refund upon notice
from IDA, an amount equal to the amount of said payment to IDA. Amounts refunded to the
Bank upon such request shall be cancelled. Although an audit of the deposit account will not
be required, IDA reserves the right to require audits at any time. The administration of this
Credit will be the responsibilityof the Ministry of Economy and Finance.




6.5     The implementation of the PRSC-1 program is not expected to have any
significant environmental impact, given the nature of the policy actions and the short-
term horizon of the operation. Implementation of the sector reforms over the three-year
PRSC series (cotton, irrigation) could potentially have some minimal environmental impact.
Potential implications in the transport sector are addressed through a requirement that the
teams undertaking road maintenance activities include an environmental specialist. For any
new irrigation activities an environmental assessment is undertaken and a mitigation plan
prepared. Implementation of the longer-term cotton sector reforms may have environmental
implications due to the potential increase in cotton production and attendant increase in
fertilizerlpesticide use. An assessment of the likely environmental impact shows, however,
that the magnitude of environmental risks strictly associated with the cotton sector is very
limited, as no major expansion of cotton cultivated areas is expected. Environmental issues
associated with the cotton privatization program will be incorporated in the
biddinglnegotiationsprocess for each companyprivatized.

6.6     The PRSC-1 program, if successful, would likely contribute to expansion of private
sector activity, inclusive of production operations in polluting sectors. These impacts will be
addressed to some extent under future Bank projects, but would best be tackled in a perennial
manner through the country's environmental management agency. Yet Mali's capacity to
guide industrial development in sustainable manner (through, for example, the review of
proposals, issuance of permits, and enforcement of standards) is currently assessed to be

weak. Consideration for providing Bank support to strengthen Mali's environment
management capacity shouldbe discussed in the context of the upcoming CAS exercise.


                                 RISKSAND RISK MITIGATION

6.7    The operation faces moderate external, political and budget risks. External risk
stems mainly from Mali's vulnerability to climatic/natural shocks (drought, avian flu),
exchange rate and commodity price fluctuations (cotton, oil) and regional developments
(persistent C6te d71voirecrisis). Mitigating these risks over the horizon of the proposed
operation remains a challenge: it depends on the economy's capacity to adjust in the wake of
the shocks, and the Government's continued ability to manage the impact of the shocks. The
operation would help strengthenboth these capacities, through policy measures that contribute
to economic growth and diversification, and hrther strengthen public finance management
within a sound macroeconomic framework. Additionally, the overall country dialogue would
allow for some budget flexibility to address crisis-related expenses within the context of a
prudently managed budget that supported priority growth and poverty reduction programs.
The introduction of predictable and timely support through the PRSC series, and continued
progress with donor coordination, will also be important elements for mitigating the external
risks.

6 3    Political risk, linked to the upcoming presidential elections in April 2007, is that the
pace of program implementation could slow or that the Government's current commitment to
some reforms (such as bank restructuring, cotton) could wane as the election approaches. This
risk, which is low to moderate, is mitigated by the front loading of the policy measures, to be
completed before submission of the Credit to the Board. The country team is monitoring this
risk on a continualbasis in order to detectproblem areas early on so as to be able to adjust the
Bank's response as needed.

6.9    Budget risk stems from ongoing financial crises in the cotton and electricityparastatal
companies (CMDT, EDM) which could potentially require government transfers to cover the
large projected deficits in those sectors. Although this risk is moderate to high, the 2007
budget includes no provision for possible transfers to these two companies: for cotton, the
Govemment indicates that the required recapitalization of CMDT (equivalent to 1.3percent of
GDP) will be covered through debt conversion rather than cash transfer (and thus with no
treasury impact); while for electricitythe Government has not yet formally acknowledged the
company's need for a cash infusion during 2007 (amounting to about 0.2 percent of GDP at
least, and 0.8 percent of government expenditures) in order to remain viable. It is thus
possible that actual implementation of the government budget during 2007 could be
significantly different from the approved budget. This risk is being mitigated through the
strengthened financial management in both companies and close monitoring of their
respective financial and cash positions. Additionally, a financial evaluation of the CMDT will
be undertaken in the first half of 2007 to better understand the nature of the losses and devise
corrective measures.

Mr. President,

I have the honor to submit this Letter of Development Policy in support of the request made
by the Government of Mali to the World Bank for a First Poverty Reduction StrategySupport
Credit (PRSC-1). The assistance requested will enable the Government to implement its
second generation poverty reduction strategy for 2007-2011. The specific reform program to
be supported by the credit covers the following areas: (i) strengthening the macroeconomic
fi-amework and public finance management; (ii) facilitating economic growth; and (iii)
improving access to basic social services.

This credit, part of a multi year program, will contribute to finding of the 2007 priority
expenditure program.

The Ministry of Economy and Finance will coordinate the implementation of the PRSC
supported actions, through a commission chaired by the SecretaryGeneral of the Ministry and
including representativesfrom other ministerial departments involved in the reform program,
such as the Cotton Sector Restructuring Mission, the Financial Sector Development Project,
Government's Action Plan for the Improving and ModernizingPublic Finance Management.

The attached documents (letter of developmentpolicy and matrix of reform measures) present
the specific objectives, strategies and action plans, and implementation timetable for each
program component to be implementedby the Government.



Very truly yours,
